    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="c6c6521364224a3e9af12c9948702070">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '37efdc1c-b261-48e9-89bf-7614f87867cd'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d6254194" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Owing to high energy capacities, transition metal chalcogenides have drawn significant research attention as the promising electrode materials for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs). However, limited cycle life and inferior rate capabilities still hinder their practical application. Improvement of the intrinsic conductivity by smart choice of elemental combination along with carbon coupling of the nanostructures may result in excellence of rate capability and prolonged cycling stability. Herein, a hierarchically porous binary transition metal selenide (Fe<sub>2</sub>CoSe<sub>4</sub>, termed as FCSe) nanomaterial with improved intrinsic conductivity was prepared through an exclusive methodology. The hierarchically porous structure, intimate nanoparticle–carbon matrix contact, and better intrinsic conductivity result in extraordinary electrochemical performance through their synergistic effect. The synthesized FCSe exhibits excellent rate capability (816.3 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup> and 400.2 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 32 A g<sup>−1</sup>), extended cycle life (350 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> even after 5000 cycles at 4 A g<sup>−1</sup>), and adequately high energy capacity (614.5 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup> after 100 cycles) as anode material for SIBs. When further combined with lab-made Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C cathode in Na-ion full cells, FCSe presents reasonably high and stable specific capacity.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma201802745-sec-0010">
         
         <p>The research on sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) has gained sufficient momentum in the last decade owing to the advantages with regard to the low cost and abundance of sodium resources.<span><a href="#adma201802745-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201802745-bib-0001_R_d6254186e391" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Chem. Soc. Rev. Energy Environ. Sci.">1</a></span> Inadequate uptake of Na ions in (even expanded) graphite limits the theoretical capacity of SIBs hindering its utilization for large-scale applications.<span><a href="#adma201802745-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201802745-bib-0002_R_d6254186e393" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Nat. Commun.">2</a></span> On the other hand, due to the multiple electron transfers per metal atom compared with only one electron per six carbon atoms, metals and metal compounds can provide higher specific capacities by alloying and conversion reactions as anode of SIBs.[<span><a href="#adma201802745-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201802745-bib-0001_R_d6254186e395" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Chem. Soc. Rev. Energy Environ. Sci.">1</a>, <a href="#adma201802745-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201802745-bib-0003_R_d6254186e395" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Chem. Commun.">3</a></span>] By realizing this fact, several new anode materials have been developed including high capacity metals (P,<span><a href="#adma201802745-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201802745-bib-0004_R_d6254186e398" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Nat Nano">4</a></span> Sn,<span><a href="#adma201802745-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201802745-bib-0005_R_d6254186e400" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Energy Environ. Sci.">5</a></span> and Sb<span><a href="#adma201802745-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201802745-bib-0006_R_d6254186e403" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Energy Environ. Sci. Energy Environ. Sci.">6</a></span>) and metal compound (oxides,<span><a href="#adma201802745-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201802745-bib-0007_R_d6254186e405" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Mater. Small Adv. Mater.">7</a></span> phosphides,<span><a href="#adma201802745-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201802745-bib-0008_R_d6254186e407" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Chem. – Eur. J.">8</a></span> sulfides,<span><a href="#adma201802745-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201802745-bib-0009_R_d6254186e409" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Mater. Nat. Commun. Energy Storage Mater. Adv. Mater. Small Sci. Rep. Nat. Commun. ACS Catal. Small">9</a></span> and selenides<span><a href="#adma201802745-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201802745-bib-0010_R_d6254186e411" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Mater. ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Adv. Energy Mater. ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Adv. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater.">10</a></span>). Among these compounds are the transition metal chalcogenides (TMCs) that have been explored extensively in recent few years due to their promising energy capacity as anode of SIBs.[<span><a href="#adma201802745-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201802745-bib-0009_R_d6254186e413" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Mater. Nat. Commun. Energy Storage Mater. Adv. Mater. Small Sci. Rep. Nat. Commun. ACS Catal. Small">9</a>-<a href="#adma201802745-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201802745-bib-0011_R_d6254186e413" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Energy Storage Mater. Energy Environ. Sci. J. Mater. Chem. A﻿﻿ J. Mater. Chem. A﻿﻿ Energy Environ. Sci. Energy Environ. Sci. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Energy Mater.">11</a></span>] However, TMCs suffer from poor cycling stability caused by the pulverization of electrodes during large volume changes occurring in conversion reactions between TMCs and Na ions.[<span><a href="#adma201802745-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201802745-bib-0001_R_d6254186e417" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Chem. Soc. Rev. Energy Environ. Sci.">1</a>, <a href="#adma201802745-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201802745-bib-0003_R_d6254186e417" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Chem. Commun.">3</a>, <a href="#adma201802745-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201802745-bib-0011_R_d6254186e417" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Energy Storage Mater. Energy Environ. Sci. J. Mater. Chem. A﻿﻿ J. Mater. Chem. A﻿﻿ Energy Environ. Sci. Energy Environ. Sci. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Energy Mater.">11</a></span>] In this respect, it is a common practice to mix carbon species with TMCs to enhance the electrical conductivity and accommodate large volume changes.[<span><a href="#adma201802745-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201802745-bib-0005_R_d6254186e420" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Energy Environ. Sci.">5</a>, <a href="#adma201802745-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201802745-bib-0009_R_d6254186e420" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Mater. Nat. Commun. Energy Storage Mater. Adv. Mater. Small Sci. Rep. Nat. Commun. ACS Catal. Small">9</a>, <a href="#adma201802745-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201802745-bib-0011_R_d6254186e420" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Energy Storage Mater. Energy Environ. Sci. J. Mater. Chem. A﻿﻿ J. Mater. Chem. A﻿﻿ Energy Environ. Sci. Energy Environ. Sci. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Energy Mater.">11</a>, <a href="#adma201802745-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201802745-bib-0012_R_d6254186e420" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Mater. J. Mater. Chem. A﻿﻿ Nanoscale">12</a></span>] For example, Huang and co-workers recently prepared a composite in which SnS nanoparticles were anchored on N-doped 3D graphene.[<span><a href="#adma201802745-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201802745-bib-0011_R_d6254186e423" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Energy Storage Mater. Energy Environ. Sci. J. Mater. Chem. A﻿﻿ J. Mater. Chem. A﻿﻿ Energy Environ. Sci. Energy Environ. Sci. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Energy Mater.">11</a></span>] Due to reduced pulverizations, this composite showed good capacity retention of 87% even up to 1000 cycle. Similarly, Peng et al. decorated cobalt sulfide nanoplates on the reduced graphene oxide (rGO) which can present reversible high capacity up to 1000 cycles.[<span><a href="#adma201802745-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201802745-bib-0009_R_d6254186e426" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Mater. Nat. Commun. Energy Storage Mater. Adv. Mater. Small Sci. Rep. Nat. Commun. ACS Catal. Small">9</a></span>] Huang et al. presented an anode of V<sub>2</sub>S<sub>5</sub> and graphite hybrid nanosheets which can show a capacity of 496 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> even up to 500 cycles. Hou et al. reported carbon spheres with embedded cobalt selenide nanoparticles which can be cycled up to 10 000 cycles. Chen's group reported FeSe<sub>2</sub> microspheres showing a stable capacity of 372 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> up to 2000 cycles.</p>
         
         <p>In a comparative study, Yu and co-workers described that selenide-based TMCs perform better than transition metal sulfides.[<span><a href="#adma201802745-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201802745-bib-0009_R_d6254186e443" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Mater. Nat. Commun. Energy Storage Mater. Adv. Mater. Small Sci. Rep. Nat. Commun. ACS Catal. Small">9</a>, <a href="#adma201802745-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201802745-bib-0013_R_d6254186e443" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Mater.">13</a></span>] Similarly, Fe-, Co-, Ni-, Sb-, and Sn-based selenides have shown comparatively better performances compared to the corresponding sulfides.[<span><a href="#adma201802745-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201802745-bib-0010_R_d6254186e446" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Mater. ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Adv. Energy Mater. ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Adv. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater.">10</a>, <a href="#adma201802745-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201802745-bib-0011_R_d6254186e446" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Energy Storage Mater. Energy Environ. Sci. J. Mater. Chem. A﻿﻿ J. Mater. Chem. A﻿﻿ Energy Environ. Sci. Energy Environ. Sci. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Energy Mater.">11</a>, <a href="#adma201802745-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201802745-bib-0014_R_d6254186e446" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Chem. – Eur. J.">14</a></span>] On the other hand, binary transition metal chalcogenides with a spinel structure of AB<sub>2</sub>X<sub>4</sub> (where A and B are transition metals and X is chalcogenide) have recently gained a widespread attention for energy storage and conversion systems.[<span><a href="#adma201802745-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201802745-bib-0003_R_d6254186e453" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Chem. Commun.">3</a>, <a href="#adma201802745-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201802745-bib-0008_R_d6254186e453" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Chem. – Eur. J.">8</a>, <a href="#adma201802745-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201802745-bib-0011_R_d6254186e453" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Energy Storage Mater. Energy Environ. Sci. J. Mater. Chem. A﻿﻿ J. Mater. Chem. A﻿﻿ Energy Environ. Sci. Energy Environ. Sci. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Energy Mater.">11</a>, <a href="#adma201802745-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201802745-bib-0015_R_d6254186e453" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Energy Mater. Nano Lett. J. Mater. Chem. A﻿﻿ ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces J. Mater. Chem. A﻿﻿">15</a></span>] These binary-metal chalcogenides benefit from large crystal size, higher conductivity, and better electrochemical activities compared with their monometal counterparts.[<span><a href="#adma201802745-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201802745-bib-0003_R_d6254186e457" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Chem. Commun.">3</a>, <a href="#adma201802745-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201802745-bib-0008_R_d6254186e457" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Chem. – Eur. J.">8</a>, <a href="#adma201802745-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201802745-bib-0009_R_d6254186e457" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Mater. Nat. Commun. Energy Storage Mater. Adv. Mater. Small Sci. Rep. Nat. Commun. ACS Catal. Small">9</a>, <a href="#adma201802745-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201802745-bib-0011_R_d6254186e457" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Energy Storage Mater. Energy Environ. Sci. J. Mater. Chem. A﻿﻿ J. Mater. Chem. A﻿﻿ Energy Environ. Sci. Energy Environ. Sci. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Energy Mater.">11</a>, <a href="#adma201802745-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201802745-bib-0015_R_d6254186e457" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Energy Mater. Nano Lett. J. Mater. Chem. A﻿﻿ ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces J. Mater. Chem. A﻿﻿">15</a>, <a href="#adma201802745-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201802745-bib-0016_R_d6254186e457" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Energy Environ. Sci. Nano Energy ACS Nano Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. J. Solid State Chem.">16</a></span>] Monometal selenides of iron and cobalt have shown promising performance as anode of SIBs.[<span><a href="#adma201802745-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201802745-bib-0009_R_d6254186e460" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Mater. Nat. Commun. Energy Storage Mater. Adv. Mater. Small Sci. Rep. Nat. Commun. ACS Catal. Small">9</a>-<a href="#adma201802745-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201802745-bib-0011_R_d6254186e460" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Energy Storage Mater. Energy Environ. Sci. J. Mater. Chem. A﻿﻿ J. Mater. Chem. A﻿﻿ Energy Environ. Sci. Energy Environ. Sci. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Energy Mater.">11</a>, <a href="#adma201802745-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201802745-bib-0017_R_d6254186e460" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Nano Res. Chem. Eng. J. Energy Storage Mater.">17</a></span>] Moreover, a combination of these two (iron and cobalt) as a binary-metal selenide will possess “metal-like conductivity.”[<span><a href="#adma201802745-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201802745-bib-0015_R_d6254186e463" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Energy Mater. Nano Lett. J. Mater. Chem. A﻿﻿ ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces J. Mater. Chem. A﻿﻿">15</a></span>] Therefore, a binary iron cobalt metal selenide will benefit from enhanced intrinsic conductivity and larger crystal size (compared to monometal selenide) which is expected to be a key factor for high rate performance and cycling stability of the SIBs. To the best of our knowledge, there is no report of an iron cobalt–based binary-metal selenide anode for SIBs.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, for the first time we have designed and synthesized hierarchically porous spheres of iron cobalt binary-metal selenide (Fe<sub>2</sub>CoSe<sub>4</sub>, termed as FCSe) using a facile hydrothermal process followed by an exclusive selenization and annealing strategy. <b>Figure</b><a href="#adma201802745-fig-0001">1</a>a illustrates the synthetic process for the preparation of the FCSe. During the hydrothermal growth reaction, the stoichiometric reactants concentration was achieved by varying the amount of cobalt precursor relative to that of iron, which lead to the growth Fe–Co–glycerate precursor (FCGP) spheres that are further comprised of nanosheets (Figure <a href="#adma201802745-fig-0001">1</a>b,c). A broad X-ray diffraction (XRD) peak around 12° was obtained for FCGP (Figure S1a, Supporting Information) which is a characteristic of metal alkoxides.[<span><a href="#adma201802745-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201802745-bib-0009_R_d6254186e479" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Mater. Nat. Commun. Energy Storage Mater. Adv. Mater. Small Sci. Rep. Nat. Commun. ACS Catal. Small">9</a></span>] In the next step, FCGP was selenized and annealed in a facile single process, as elaborated in Figure S1b (Supporting Information). Initially powder selenium (Se-P) and FCGP were loaded in a ceramic boat. With increasing temperature (to 300 °C), Se-P get melted and reacts with incoming H<sub>2</sub> gas to form H<sub>2</sub>Se locally (i.e., H<sub>2</sub> + Se → H<sub>2</sub>Se). H<sub>2</sub>Se is a strong reducing agent, so it swiftly reduces the FCGP and converts Fe/Co glycerate into Fe/Co selenides. After the selenization process, system temperature was increased slowly and selenized product was annealed at 400 °C under N<sub>2</sub> environment. This annealing process enhances the crystallization and helps to improve the phase purity of the final product. As there was the possibility of crystal growth which would result in larger particles and reduced surface area, so the annealing duration was optimized to 1.5 h. Finally, we obtained hierarchically porous FCSe spheres, which are in turn composed of nanoparticles (Figure <a href="#adma201802745-fig-0001">1</a>d,e).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma201802745-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/76795469-a4a7-47c6-accb-9d338f36c5ce/adma201802745-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/76795469-a4a7-47c6-accb-9d338f36c5ce/adma201802745-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a06c9f1b-9d88-4277-8ddb-d9e40fd4daf8/adma201802745-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/76795469-a4a7-47c6-accb-9d338f36c5ce/adma201802745-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma201802745-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.201802745" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Schematic illustration of the formation process of Fe<sub>2</sub>CoSe<sub>4</sub> (FCSe). Hydrothermal treatment results in Fe–Co–glycerate precursor spheres made of nanosheets and then combined selenization and annealing produce hierarchically porous FCSe spheres made of nanoparticles. SEM and TEM images of b,c) FCGP sphere and c,d) FCSe sphere, respectively.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>When iron nitrate alone was used as the starting material, the final product contains iron selenide (JCPDS Card No. 75-0608 and 85-0735) and elemental iron (JCPDS Card No. 01-1262), as depicted by XRD spectra in Figure S2a (Supporting Information). By introducing 10% cobalt nitrate in the starting materials, peaks of FCSe (Fe<sub>2</sub>CoSe<sub>4</sub>, JCPDS Card No. 89-1967) appear in the XRD spectrum, in addition to iron selenide (JCPDS Card No. 85-0735) and iron (JCPDS Card No. 01-1262) peaks (Figure S2b, Supporting Information). Shift in FCSe peaks to the higher diffraction angles can be attributed to the doping of excessive iron in the FCSe crystal. It is worth mentioning that the addition of 25% cobalt nitrate results in the formation of a stoichiometric phase, and all of the diffraction peaks are associated with the single phase FCSe (JCPDS Card No. 89-1967, Figure S2c, Supporting Information). Further increase in concentration of cobalt nitrate to 50% or 100% results in nonstoichiometric reactants, thus leads to formation of cobalt selenide (JCPDS Card No. 89-2004) and elemental cobalt (JCPDS Card No. 01-1255), as shown in Figure S2d,e (Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <p>Low-magnification scanning electron microscope (SEM) images in Figure S3a,b (Supporting Information) revealed uniform morphologies of FCGP and FCSe. Moreover, high-magnification SEM and transmission electron microscope (TEM) analysis showed that FCGP (Figure <a href="#adma201802745-fig-0001">1</a>b,c) and FCSe spheres (Figure <a href="#adma201802745-fig-0001">1</a>d,e) possess average diameters of 300 and 260 nm, respectively. The FCGP spheres were comprised of nanosheets which get converted into small particles during the annealing process and thus hierarchically porous structure of the FCSe spheres was obtained. X-ray diffraction analysis (Figure S4c, Supporting Information) shows that monoclinic FCSe (Fe<sub>2</sub>CoSe<sub>4</sub>, JCPDS Card No. 89-1967) belonging to the space group <i>l</i><sub>2</sub>/<i>m</i> has been successfully synthesized. For comparison of electrochemical properties, iron cobalt sulfide (FCS) and iron cobalt oxide (FCO) were produced using the same FCGP by heating with sulfur powder in Ar/H<sub>2</sub> (H<sub>2</sub> 10 vol%) and in air, respectively. It is worth pointing out that the morphology of the FCGP spheres remains uniform during the sulfidation and oxidation process to produce FCS (Figure S4d–f, Supporting Information) and FCO (Figure S4g–i, Supporting Information), respectively. Moreover, the XRD results confirm the formation of FCS (JCPDS Card Nos. 79-0598 and 29-0484) and FCO (Fe<sub>2</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub>, JCPDS Card No. 79-1744). This evidences the success of our proposed strategy to produce binary-metal chalcogenides in a simplistic way. HRTEM images of a typical FCSe sphere (Figure S5, Supporting Information) depict that the nanoparticles of FCSe are held together by a continuous carbon matrix. This amorphous carbon originates from the glycerol used in precursors. It should be noted that nanoparticles of FCSe present clear lattice fringes (<b>Figure</b><a href="#adma201802745-fig-0002">2</a>a) with <i>d =</i> 0.264 nm corresponding to the plane (202) and <i>d =</i> 0.307 nm for the plane (200) which are consistent with XRD results. Distribution of elements (Fe, Co, Se, and C) across a single FCSe sphere was analyzed using high-resolution EDX elemental mapping analysis in scanning/transmission electron microscope (S/TEM). These EDX maps (Figure <a href="#adma201802745-fig-0002">2</a>b–f) confirm the uniform distribution of Fe, Co, Se, and C across the sphere. Moreover, there exists no evidence of stray islands/crystallites of the elemental Se, which confirms the successful selenization of the Fe–Co glycerate precursor. Thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) confirms the weight losses of ≈29.6 and ≈30.2 wt% (Figure S11, Supporting Information) for FCSe and FCS which can be attributed to the carbon content present in both materials.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma201802745-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/f72ac7f3-d63e-4de1-963b-3ba6f9c5621a/adma201802745-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/f72ac7f3-d63e-4de1-963b-3ba6f9c5621a/adma201802745-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/d20a20fa-8bd7-42d2-9104-730900b0eb65/adma201802745-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/f72ac7f3-d63e-4de1-963b-3ba6f9c5621a/adma201802745-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma201802745-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.201802745" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) HRTEM analysis of a typical sphere of FCSe b–f) high-resolution EDX elemental mapping images of a single sphere of FCSe. N<sub>2</sub> adsorption/desorption isotherms for g) FCGP and h) FCSe. Corresponding pore size distributions (inset) were calculated by the density functional theory method.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The porosity of the FCGP, FCSe, FCS, and FCO spheres was analyzed through nitrogen adsorption/desorption tests. Isotherms (as shown in Figure <a href="#adma201802745-fig-0002">2</a>g,h and Figure S6 in the Supporting Information) can be categorized as of type IV with distinctive hysteresis loop presented in the higher-pressure range that confirms the mesoporous characteristics the synthesized nanomaterials.[<span><a href="#adma201802745-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201802745-bib-0009_R_d6254186e585" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Mater. Nat. Commun. Energy Storage Mater. Adv. Mater. Small Sci. Rep. Nat. Commun. ACS Catal. Small">9</a>, <a href="#adma201802745-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201802745-bib-0015_R_d6254186e585" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Energy Mater. Nano Lett. J. Mater. Chem. A﻿﻿ ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces J. Mater. Chem. A﻿﻿">15</a>, <a href="#adma201802745-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201802745-bib-0018_R_d6254186e585" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Energy Mater.">18</a></span>] The FCSe spheres exhibited highest Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) surface area (81.21 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>) as compared to the FCS (71.90 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>), FCO (59.74 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>), and FCGP (28.78 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>). Pore size distribution (Figure <a href="#adma201802745-fig-0002">2</a>h, inset) calculated by density functional theory (DFT) procedure reveals the presence of mesopores with size distribution centered below 5.0 nm for the FCSe sample. Interestingly, the pores with 10 nm width contribute 89.46% of the total pore volume in FCSe. The mesoporous nature and large surface area of FCSe can not only enhance the Na-ion surface interactions but also boost up the electronic conductivity by shortening ionic diffusion lengths and hence play an important role in the improved electrochemical performance of the SIBs.</p>
         
         <p>The elemental composition and oxidation states of the FCSe were further analyzed through X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) analysis. XPS survey spectrum (Figure S7a, Supporting Information) reveals the presence of the elements Fe, Co, Se, C, and O in FCSe spheres, where oxygen peaks originate from the inevitable surface oxidation. High-resolution spectrum of Fe 2p in Figure S7b (Supporting Information) shows two peaks located at binding energies 711.8 and 724.8 eV corresponding to Fe 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and Fe 2p<sub>1/2</sub>, respectively, along with two satellite peaks (indicated as “Sat.”). The characteristic energy difference of 13 eV between Fe 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and Fe 2p<sub>1/2</sub> peaks is a characteristics of the Fe<sup>3+</sup> state.[<span><a href="#adma201802745-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201802745-bib-0015_R_d6254186e621" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Energy Mater. Nano Lett. J. Mater. Chem. A﻿﻿ ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces J. Mater. Chem. A﻿﻿">15</a>, <a href="#adma201802745-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201802745-bib-0019_R_d6254186e621" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Energy Environ. Sci.">19</a></span>] Curve-fitted core level spectrum of Co 2p (Figure S7c, Supporting Information) shows two regions Co 2p<sub>1/2</sub> and Co 2p<sub>3/2</sub>, each of which can be best fitted with two spin–orbit doublets, characteristic of Co<sup>2+</sup> and Co<sup>3+</sup>, and a shake-up satellite.[<span><a href="#adma201802745-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201802745-bib-0015_R_d6254186e632" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Energy Mater. Nano Lett. J. Mater. Chem. A﻿﻿ ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces J. Mater. Chem. A﻿﻿">15</a></span>] Spectrum of Se 3d (Figure S7d, Supporting Information) can be well fitted with two peaks of Se 3d<sub>3/2</sub> and Se 3d<sub>5/2</sub>. These XPS results depict that the surface of the FCSe sample has a composition of Fe<sup>3+</sup>, Co<sup>2+</sup>, Co<sup>3+</sup>, and Se<sup>2−</sup> species which is in good agreement with the literature.[<span><a href="#adma201802745-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201802745-bib-0015_R_d6254186e649" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Energy Mater. Nano Lett. J. Mater. Chem. A﻿﻿ ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces J. Mater. Chem. A﻿﻿">15</a>, <a href="#adma201802745-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201802745-bib-0019_R_d6254186e649" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Energy Environ. Sci.">19</a></span>]</p>
         
         <p>The sodium storage properties of FCSe, FCS, and FCO anodes were evaluated by cyclic voltammetry (CV) and galvanostatic charge/discharge in half-cell configuration by assembling CR2032-type cells with Na foil as counterelectrode. <b>Figure</b><a href="#adma201802745-fig-0003">3</a>a shows the first four CV curves of FCSe anode in the 0.5–3.0 V voltage range at a scan rate of 0.5 mV S<sup>−1</sup>. Like other TMCs, a sharp reduction peak at 0.93 V in the first cycle can be associated with the formation of Na<sub>2</sub>Se and nanocrystals of transition metal (iron and cobalt).[<span><a href="#adma201802745-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201802745-bib-0014_R_d6254186e663" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Chem. – Eur. J.">14</a>, <a href="#adma201802745-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201802745-bib-0020_R_d6254186e663" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Nat. Commun.">20</a></span>] In the following sodiation cycles, three stable redox peaks appear at the fixed voltage values, i.e., 1.42, 1.16, and 0.66 V, respectively. Except the first cycle, the total area under reduction and oxidation peaks is approximately same in all cycles, implying reversible and stable cycling performance of the FCSe anode. Figure <a href="#adma201802745-fig-0003">3</a>b shows a high initial discharge capacity (750 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>) of the FCSe anode at a current density of 1 A g<sup>−1</sup> in the 0.5–3.0 V voltage range. With an initial capacity loss of 13.17%, succeeding cycles show overlapping charge/discharge profiles (649.5 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>) and all charge/discharge plateaus are in good agreement with CV results.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma201802745-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ee902931-fe4d-4b03-8313-c19ad21227dc/adma201802745-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/ee902931-fe4d-4b03-8313-c19ad21227dc/adma201802745-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/79ba645e-9c77-41d0-ad7f-7d0e95fe288c/adma201802745-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/ee902931-fe4d-4b03-8313-c19ad21227dc/adma201802745-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma201802745-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.201802745" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) CV curves of the FCSe anode in sodium half-cells for first four cycles at a scan rate of 0.5 mV S<sup>−1</sup> in the 0.5–3.0 V range, and b) electrochemical discharge and charge profiles (between 0.5 and 3.0 V) of the FCSe anode at a current rate of 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">To elucidate the reaction mechanism, CV curves are discussed in detail here. Three peaks (at 1.42, 1.16, and 0.66 V) can be attributed to the multistep transformation of metal selenide to Na<sub>2</sub>Se due to Na-ion insertion.[<span><a href="#adma201802745-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201802745-bib-0010_R_d6254186e701" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Mater. ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Adv. Energy Mater. ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Adv. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater.">10</a>, <a href="#adma201802745-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201802745-bib-0021_R_d6254186e701" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Energy Environ. Sci.">21</a></span>] The peak at 1.42 V can be ascribed to the insertion of Na<sup>+</sup> ions into Fe<sub>2</sub>CoSe<sub>4</sub> to form the intermediate product Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>Fe<sub>2</sub>CoSe<sub>4</sub>. Further insertion of Na<sup>+</sup> ions leads to the loss of selenium from Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>Fe<sub>2</sub>CoSe<sub>4</sub> to result Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>Fe<sub>2</sub>CoSe<sub>4−</sub><i><sub>y</sub></i> and Na<sub>2</sub>Se at 1.16 V. Finally, Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>Fe<sub>2</sub>CoSe<sub>4−</sub><i><sub>y</sub></i> reacts with Na<sup>+</sup> ions at 0.66 V and gets converted to Na<sub>2</sub>Se and nanoparticles of iron and cobalt.[<span><a href="#adma201802745-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201802745-bib-0011_R_d6254186e754" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Energy Storage Mater. Energy Environ. Sci. J. Mater. Chem. A﻿﻿ J. Mater. Chem. A﻿﻿ Energy Environ. Sci. Energy Environ. Sci. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Energy Mater.">11</a>, <a href="#adma201802745-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201802745-bib-0021_R_d6254186e754" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Energy Environ. Sci.">21</a></span>] These transformation step can be written as follows
            <div class="inline-equation" id="adma201802745-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><img class="" src="/cms/asset/56f490f6-26bf-4545-8a4d-982017f51bb6/adma201802745-math-0001.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:adma201802745:adma201802745-math-0001" title="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:adma201802745:adma201802745-math-0001" loading="lazy"></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>
            <div class="inline-equation" id="adma201802745-disp-0002"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><img class="" src="/cms/asset/feeefb7c-f4ec-4035-90f9-e266008676c3/adma201802745-math-0002.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:adma201802745:adma201802745-math-0002" title="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:adma201802745:adma201802745-math-0002" loading="lazy"></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(2)</span></div>
            <div class="inline-equation" id="adma201802745-disp-0003"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><img class="" src="/cms/asset/b713736b-8fe7-4128-8f10-1857f09a6ba1/adma201802745-math-0003.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:adma201802745:adma201802745-math-0003" title="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:adma201802745:adma201802745-math-0003" loading="lazy"></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(3)</span></div>
         </div>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">Variation in the first and subsequent cycles can be assigned to the solid electrolyte interlayer formation and structural rearrangements.[<span><a href="#adma201802745-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201802745-bib-0010_R_d6254186e766" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Mater. ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Adv. Energy Mater. ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Adv. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater.">10</a>, <a href="#adma201802745-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201802745-bib-0014_R_d6254186e766" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Chem. – Eur. J.">14</a>, <a href="#adma201802745-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201802745-bib-0021_R_d6254186e766" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Energy Environ. Sci.">21</a></span>] Two overlapping oxidation peaks (at 1.82 and 1.93 V) in the first scan and a single oxidation peak (at 1.86 V) in all subsequent scans result from the desodiation reaction of Na<sub>2</sub>Se.[<span><a href="#adma201802745-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201802745-bib-0009_R_d6254186e771" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Mater. Nat. Commun. Energy Storage Mater. Adv. Mater. Small Sci. Rep. Nat. Commun. ACS Catal. Small">9</a>, <a href="#adma201802745-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201802745-bib-0010_R_d6254186e771" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Mater. ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Adv. Energy Mater. ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Adv. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater.">10</a>, <a href="#adma201802745-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201802745-bib-0020_R_d6254186e771" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Nat. Commun.">20</a></span>] During this reaction, metallic (iron and cobalt) nanoparticles react with Na<sub>2</sub>Se and regenerate the FCSe material species. Reaction can be summarized as
            <div class="inline-equation" id="adma201802745-disp-0004"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><img class="" src="/cms/asset/03105470-ec18-4e8a-af7c-58a64072069c/adma201802745-math-0004.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:adma201802745:adma201802745-math-0004" title="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:adma201802745:adma201802745-math-0004" loading="lazy"></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(4)</span></div>
         </div>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">To confirm the identity of charge/discharge redox products and hence explain the reaction mechanism of FCSe, ex situ XRD, selected area electron diffraction (SAED) and HRTEM were carried out after 100 cycles. <b>Figure</b><a href="#adma201802745-fig-0004">4</a>a shows the XRD patterns of FCSe electrode at the different charge/discharge states, as indicated in the corresponding voltage profile. Peaks located at 43.2° and 50.4° are assigned to current collector (Cu) in every pattern of tested samples. At fully charged state (I), only the characteristic peaks of FCSe (Fe<sub>2</sub>CoSe<sub>4</sub>, JCPDS Card No. 89-1967) are observed. With a partial discharge to 1.45 and 0.9 V (II, III), the peaks ascribing to Na<sub>2</sub>Se (JCPDS Card No. 47-1699), Fe (JCPDS Card No. 87-0722), and Co (JCPDS Card No. 89-4307) start to appear with FCSe. At fully discharged state 0.5 V (IV), the characteristic peaks of Na<sub>2</sub>Se at 37.4° and 44.1° are fully apparent. SAED analysis carried out for the same samples further confirms the charge/discharge redox products by several discernible concentric rings composed of discrete diffraction points. Rings of the SAED pattern in Figure <a href="#adma201802745-fig-0004">4</a>b can be assigned to FCSe and graphitic carbon taken at 3.0V (I). When partially discharged to (II and III), the reflection spots of Na<sub>2</sub>Se start to appear along with FCSe and elemental Fe/Co species (Figure <a href="#adma201802745-fig-0004">4</a>c,d). The SAED pattern taken at fully discharged state 0.5 V (IV) presents the reflections only ascribing to Na<sub>2</sub>Se (Figure <a href="#adma201802745-fig-0004">4</a>e). During the charging process, at 2.2 V (V), the FCSe phase reappears (Figure <a href="#adma201802745-fig-0004">4</a>f) confirming the reversible reaction mechanism that can be denoted by the following equation
            <div class="inline-equation" id="adma201802745-disp-0005"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><img class="" src="/cms/asset/ace77d05-8c90-47ca-b531-00a519be80f7/adma201802745-math-0005.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:adma201802745:adma201802745-math-0005" title="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:adma201802745:adma201802745-math-0005" loading="lazy"></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(5)</span></div>
         </div>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma201802745-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/4fd7807a-d5df-4110-83bb-7d62cb34a7c2/adma201802745-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/4fd7807a-d5df-4110-83bb-7d62cb34a7c2/adma201802745-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/6980c068-9515-4851-a3f6-88a8baafe37d/adma201802745-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/4fd7807a-d5df-4110-83bb-7d62cb34a7c2/adma201802745-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma201802745-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.201802745" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Ex situ XRD patterns and b–f) SAED patterns of FCSe electrodes at different charge/discharge states as indicated in the corresponding voltage profile: I) fully charged to 3.0 V, II,III) partially discharged to 1.45 V and 0.9 V, IV) fully discharged to 0.5 V, and V) partially charged to 2.2 V.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Comparison of CV and charge/discharge profiles of FCSe with FCS and FCO is presented in Figure S8 (Supporting Information). Due to better intrinsic conductivity and larger electrolyte to electrode surface area, FCSe shows much higher initial discharge capacity (750 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>) compared to FCS (393 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>) and FCO (391 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>) even at a low current density of 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>. After the first cycle, FCS and FCO both show that overlapping profiles and CV peaks are in good agreement with charge/discharge plateaus although the specific capacities are inferior as compared to FCSe. Figure S8g (Supporting Information) shows that the discharge capacities of FCSe, FCS, and FCO are 614.5, 335.8, and 533.1 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, after 100 cycles with a retention of 94.6%, 70.84% and 97.68% relative to the second cycle (at a current rate of 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>). It is worth mentioning that the specific capacity of FCSe (binary-metal selenide) after 100 cycles is better than the reported Fe/Co-based monometal selenides (Table S1, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <p>In addition to high capacities, FCSe anodes show excellent rate performance. <b>Figure</b><a href="#adma201802745-fig-0005">5</a>a compares the rate capability of FCSe, FCS, and FCO investigated at various current densities ranging from 0.5 to 32 A g<sup>−1</sup>. Remarkably, the FCSe anode can deliver reversible specific capacities of 454.4 and 400.2 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> even at ultrahigh current rates of 16 and 32 A g<sup>−1</sup> which points toward the rapid transfer of ions and electrons through FCSe. When the current densities were reversely varied from 32 to 16, 8.0, 4.0, 2.0, 1.0, and 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the specific capacities were fully recovered to 458.8, 549.1, 625.7, 689.1, 750.4, and 809.3 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Figure <a href="#adma201802745-fig-0005">5</a>b shows the charge/discharge profiles at various increasing current densities and signifies the exceptional recovery of specific capacity (dotted lines) upon reversing the current rates. Decrease in capacities at ultrahigh current rates can be associated with the voltage drop, which reached ≈1 V at 16 and 32 A g<sup>−1</sup>. Excellent rate performance can be attributed to the rapid transfer of ions and enhanced electronic conductivity, which can be verified by electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS).<span><a href="#adma201802745-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201802745-bib-0018_R_d6254186e862" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Energy Mater.">18</a></span> Nyquist plots (as shown in Figure S9 in the Supporting Information) illustrate that FCSe-based anode presents relatively short and steep inclination in the low-frequency region while relatively small diameter semicircle in the high frequency region, which is indicative of higher conductivity and lower charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) of FCSe as compared to FCS and FCO. The charge transfer resistance values of FCSe, FCS, and FCO are only 2.85, 4.1, and 6.3 Ω, respectively, which are significantly lower than the reported values of iron/cobalt monometal selenides/sulfides/oxides tested under similar conditions.[<span><a href="#adma201802745-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201802745-bib-0009_R_d6254186e868" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Mater. Nat. Commun. Energy Storage Mater. Adv. Mater. Small Sci. Rep. Nat. Commun. ACS Catal. Small">9</a>-<a href="#adma201802745-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201802745-bib-0011_R_d6254186e868" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Energy Storage Mater. Energy Environ. Sci. J. Mater. Chem. A﻿﻿ J. Mater. Chem. A﻿﻿ Energy Environ. Sci. Energy Environ. Sci. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Energy Mater.">11</a>, <a href="#adma201802745-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201802745-bib-0017_R_d6254186e868" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Nano Res. Chem. Eng. J. Energy Storage Mater.">17</a>, <a href="#adma201802745-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201802745-bib-0022_R_d6254186e868" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Energy Environ. Sci.">22</a></span>].</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma201802745-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/9dc79e0d-54f3-4440-9092-848b69db28ba/adma201802745-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/9dc79e0d-54f3-4440-9092-848b69db28ba/adma201802745-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/399f0d5d-87ef-494a-930b-7b0aeff664c7/adma201802745-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/9dc79e0d-54f3-4440-9092-848b69db28ba/adma201802745-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma201802745-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.201802745" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Rate performances of FCSe, FCS, and FCO at various current densities ranging from 0.5 to 32 A g<sup>−1</sup> and b) charge/discharge profiles at corresponding current densities (solid lines correspond to increasing current densities while dotted lines are for decreasing), and c) comparison of cycling performances of FCSe, FCS, and FCO at a current density of 4 A g<sup>−1</sup>.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Ultralong cycle life of FCSe FCS and FCO was assessed at a current density of 4 A g<sup>−1</sup> as shown in Figure <a href="#adma201802745-fig-0005">5</a>c. FCSe exhibits a stable and reversible discharge capacity of 350.9 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> even after 5000 cycles. Moreover, with few exceptions, Coulombic efficiency always stayed between 97.76% and 102.07%. The fluctuation in cycling curves of FCO and FCS (Figure <a href="#adma201802745-fig-0005">5</a>c) can be associated with the inferior columbic efficiency and electronic conductivities of these materials. EIS results have indicated higher conductivity and lower charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) of FCSe as compared to FCS and FCO. Moreover, lesser surface areas of FCO and FCS lead to the slower ions transport by increased ionic diffusion lengths compared to FCSe. These two results indicate deprived columbic efficiencies of FCO and FCS, which lead to the fluctuations in cycling curves. To prove the integrity of the electrode and the structural stability of the FCSe material, SEM and TEM analysis was also carried out in addition to above-discussed ex situ XRD and SAED. Figure S10 (Supporting Information) verifies that the FCSe material was able to maintain the spherical shapes even after 100 charge/discharge cycles. Prolonged cycling stability of FCSe can be attributed to its robust structure and enhanced intrinsic conductivity. <b>Figure</b><a href="#adma201802745-fig-0006">6</a> provides a comparison of the rate performance and cycling stability of FCSe (binary-metal selenide) with previously reported Fe/Co-based monometal selenides. It can be seen that the rate capability and cycling performances of FCSe anode are much better than any of the reported monometal selenides of iron or cobalt.[<span><a href="#adma201802745-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201802745-bib-0009_R_d6254186e911" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Mater. Nat. Commun. Energy Storage Mater. Adv. Mater. Small Sci. Rep. Nat. Commun. ACS Catal. Small">9</a>-<a href="#adma201802745-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201802745-bib-0011_R_d6254186e911" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Energy Storage Mater. Energy Environ. Sci. J. Mater. Chem. A﻿﻿ J. Mater. Chem. A﻿﻿ Energy Environ. Sci. Energy Environ. Sci. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Energy Mater.">11</a>, <a href="#adma201802745-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201802745-bib-0017_R_d6254186e911" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Nano Res. Chem. Eng. J. Energy Storage Mater.">17</a></span>] However, FCO and FCS synthesized in this study show significant cycling stability for the first 2000 cycles (i.e., 332.8 and 181.1 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively) but afterward the capacities decrease rapidly as compared to FCSe. Lower retention of FCS than those of FCSe and FCO is not clear exactly but it can be attributed to its inferior phase purity, lower structural stability, and formation of irreversible sodium polysulfides that cause the permanent capacity decay.<span><a href="#adma201802745-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201802745-bib-0013_R_d6254186e916" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Mater.">13</a></span></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma201802745-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ced6c2bf-673a-4123-9981-7b66ed341961/adma201802745-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/ced6c2bf-673a-4123-9981-7b66ed341961/adma201802745-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/fcea2a6d-da97-4d79-817d-499e1dc98dbe/adma201802745-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/ced6c2bf-673a-4123-9981-7b66ed341961/adma201802745-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma201802745-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.201802745" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Comparisons between FCSe (our work) and reported iron- or cobalt-based monometal selenides for a) rate performances and b) cycling stability.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Following the outstanding performance of FCSe in half-cell configurations, it was further utilized to fabricate Na-ion full cells with Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C (NVP/C) as cathode material. <b>Figure</b><a href="#adma201802745-fig-0007">7</a>a presents the schematic of illustration of a Na-ion full cells (FCSe/NVP full cells). As-prepared NVP/C showed NASICON-type crystal structure,<span><a href="#adma201802745-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201802745-bib-0023_R_d6254186e948" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - J. Electroanal. Chem.">23</a></span> uniform morphology, and appreciable electrochemical performance in sodium half-cells (Figure S11, Supporting Information). NVP/C delivered a reversible specific capacity of 82.8 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> with charge/discharge plateaus located at 3.25/3.45 V (Figure S11d,e, Supporting Information). The ratio of cathode to anode mass loading was kept to 5 and the voltage window of 0.5–3.0 V was applied to measure electrochemical performance of the NVP/FCSe full cells. Specific capacities were calculated on the bases of the FCSe positive electrode mass. NVP/FCSe full cells delivered a remarkable discharge capacity of 360.2 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a href="#adma201802745-fig-0007">7</a>b), with an average output voltage of 1.75 V at a current density of 0.2 A g<sup>−1</sup> (capacity based on the total weight of positive and negative electrodes is over 100 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>). Moreover, the NVP/FCSe full cell exhibited good rate capability with discharge capacities of 314.5, 224.7, 185.3, 150.0, and 105.5 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at current densities of 0.5, 1.0, 2.0, 5.0, and 8 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively (Figure <a href="#adma201802745-fig-0007">7</a>c). Interestingly, reversible specific capacity still reaches 247.6 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> even after 100 cycles (Figure <a href="#adma201802745-fig-0007">7</a>d). As shown in Figure <a href="#adma201802745-fig-0007">7</a>e, the NVP/FCSe full cell is able to power an array of commercial red light-emitting diodes (LEDs) even when the cell has already completed 100 charge discharge cycles. These results indicate that FCSe possesses great potential for practical application in SIBs.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma201802745-fig-0007"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/61300894-2fea-4418-be0e-1485bbcf9916/adma201802745-fig-0007-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/61300894-2fea-4418-be0e-1485bbcf9916/adma201802745-fig-0007-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f62cecb2-a20a-4735-bdd9-87d99e44f43f/adma201802745-fig-0007-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/61300894-2fea-4418-be0e-1485bbcf9916/adma201802745-fig-0007-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 7<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma201802745-fig-0007&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.201802745" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Schematic of illustration Na-ion full-cell assembled with FCSe as anode and NVP/C as cathode. Electrochemical performance evaluations of FCSe/NVP full cells were based on the mass of the FCSe anode; b) charge/discharge profiles of at a current density of 0.2 A g<sup>−1</sup>; c) charge/discharge profiles at different current rates and d) cycling performance of FCSe/NVP full cell at a current density of 0.2 A g<sup>−1</sup>. e) An array of commercial red light-emitting diodes powered by the NVP/FCSe full cell.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>In summary, hierarchically porous structure of FCSe (binary-metal selenide) spheres were designed and fabricated using a facile hydrothermal technique followed by a single-step selenization and annealing strategy. The structural evaluations prove that FCSe spheres produced by this method have uniform morphology, possess high porosity, and consist of nanoparticles assembled in a thin carbon matrix. Owing to these structural features and enhanced conductivity from carbon matrix and smart binary-metal selenide combination, FCSe show high reversible capacity of 614.5 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> after 100 cycles (at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>), unprecedented rate capability (i.e., 816.3 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup> and 400.2 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at an extremely high current rate of 32 A g<sup>−1</sup>), and stable cycling performance (350.9 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 4 A g<sup>−1</sup>) up to 5000 cycles. When paired with Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C in Na-ion full battery, Fe<sub>2</sub>CoSe<sub>4</sub> delivered reasonably high capacity of 360.2 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> with a retention of 247.6 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> after 100 cycles (at 0.2 A g<sup>−1</sup>) in the 0.5–3.0 V voltage range. Overall, the synthesis strategy developed in this study will open up a new way to produce transition metal–based binary-metal selenides for high-rate capability and long-term cyclability for next-generation SIBs.</p>
         </section>
